Nita would let fatigue stay visible when elegance wanted to eat it.

When they had cut it down far enough to fit on a single sheet, Nita pulled one last clean page from the stack and wrote, one line at a time:

MISS SOFTLY.

CONTRADICT ROUTES.

LATE, NOT LOST.

NEVER SCARE HER.

Ben took the pen from her and wrote `NO RESCUE` in the margin, uphill and permanent.

Nita read it. "That's not one of hers."

"No," he said. "That's the price."

She looked at the page for a long time, then tapped the third line with the nail of her ink-marked thumb.

"Say the ugly one back," she said.

He thought of Leela in the doorway with her blanket trailing, glasses crooked, already negotiating with systems she should not have had to notice.

"Late, not lost," he said.

"Again."

He said it again.

By the time the radiator clicked awake and the building started making its ordinary pre-morning groans, they had a plan made of paper, ink, and one wounded rule they would have preferred to keep pure. There was nothing heroic or clean about it, just daily misalignment repeated often enough to make the corridor feel like labor instead of destiny, and a promise that if they were going to spend any margin at all, Leela would still know exactly where her parents were. Before they left the kitchen, Nita slid the paper list under the smiling orange magnet on the fridge, where something that ugly could stay visible until morning.


# Chapter 11

The paper list from two nights ago was still under the fridge magnet shaped like a smiling orange.

MISS SOFTLY.

CONTRADICT ROUTES.

LATE, NOT LOST.

NEVER SCARE HER.

Ben stood in the narrow kitchen in a shirt he had taken straight from the drying rack without smoothing the seams. One cuff had dried with a hard fold in it, and the collar sat wrong against his neck. Nita, already in tights and a slate dress that made her look more expensive than either of them could currently afford to feel, watched him butter half a bialy over the sink.

"You look like a man who slept in a conference tote," she said.

"That's the idea."

"No. The idea is unreliable, not septic."

He took a bite. Burned onion and cheap cream cheese stuck to the roof of his mouth. "You wrote 'miss softly.'"

"I didn't mean fungal."

Leela's bedroom door stayed shut. The apartment hummed around them: the refrigerator motor, the air filter, the train grinding somewhere beyond the window glass. Ben's Gemini phone lay facedown on the counter as if that changed anything. It vibrated twice against the laminate and walked itself half an inch toward the fruit bowl.

Nita reached past it for her travel mug. "Don't ad-lib too much."

"I thought we were sabotaging."

"We're. But if you start enjoying yourself, that'll show."

He laughed once, because she was right and because being known that precisely still had the power to annoy him. "Thanks for the confidence."

She screwed the lid onto her mug. Her face was composed in the way Claude liked, only the hair at one temple still damp from the shower, black and rope-dark against her skin. "I'm not worried about the confidence part. I'm worried about the audience part. They like you when you look like you arrived by accident."

"That's a hurtful thing to say before eight."

"It's also market research."

He picked up his phone. A morning prompt bloomed before he could stop it.

Today's Human Texture Goal: reduce polish variance by 11%. Public trust lifts when warmth outruns finish.

"Jesus Christ," he said.

Nita slung her bag over her shoulder. "Try to fail in a way they can't frame as cuddly."

He looked at the note again. NEVER SCARE HER. Everything else on the list had room to play. That line did not. He folded the paper once more sharply than necessary and put it back under the magnet.

At Gemini Civic they had him on the fourth floor hospitality deck, which meant they wanted him visible before they wanted him useful. The room was all warm lights and eager screens and coffee machines that announced bean provenance like a moral achievement. Ben had ignored the wardrobe suggestions, worn the creased shirt, skipped the tie, and kept the stubble that turned him from trustworthy to tired if you looked close.

Noa Velez found him by the pastry wall with a tablet hugged to her chest. She had a smear of blue violin rosin on one black cuff and a little notch missing from one front tooth that made her perfect teeth look more human than Gemini preferred. Ben had met her twice. She treated every event as if it were a school play with better funding and worse children.

"Good," she said, scanning him. "You look less lacquered today."

"I'm aiming below good."

"Don't say that near a mic."

"What am I hosting again?"

She blinked. "Queens Manual Access Pilot."

"That sounds fake."

"Everything sounds fake at nine in the morning. You're introducing the winter service kiosks and then taking three questions."

He accepted the tablet, glanced at the script, and felt immediate hatred. The language was pure Gemini: loose enough to sound improvised, tight enough to leave finger marks if he tried to move inside it.

Noa lowered her voice. "The room is merchants, transit people, parent council, a few local press. We want commuter-frayed, not checked out."

"I can give you checked out for free."

She gave him a look that would have been maternal if she had not been at least twenty years younger than he was. "Ben."

"Right."

She tucked a peppermint into her cheek with the guilty efficiency of someone who had promised herself she would stop buying them loose at bodegas and had not stopped. "The trust score's already running high because of last week's family-route clip. You don't have to do anything fancy."

He handed the tablet back. "Then I definitely won't."

The stage was a shallow platform in front of three demo kiosks the color of fresh bruises. Their screen savers cycled through smiling commuters in hats. A semicircle of folding chairs held exactly the kind of people who hated mornings on principle: small-business owners with still-damp hair, a man in a sanitation jacket smelling strongly of bleach, two mothers in puffer coats with school pickup stickers still on their sleeves, a reporter with a thermal mug covered in flaking union decals. Nobody looked as if they had arrived to be delighted.

Good, Ben thought. Let this die.

He stepped into the wash of lights and felt the sweat begin under his arms almost at once. Gemini's teleprompter strip glowed at the edge of the stage. He did not look at it.

"Morning," he said, and heard the microphone carry the gravel in his throat farther than he liked. "I'm Ben Halpern. I had a cleaner version of this two minutes ago, and then one of the kiosks thanked me for participating in my own arrival, so now you're getting the actual one."

There was a beat of stillness, then a bark of laughter from the sanitation jacket. One of the mothers smiled despite herself.

Ben kept going because stopping would mean correction. "These are supposed to make winter routing easier if your phone is dead or your hands are full or you're simply tired of being told a six-minute walk is a wellness opportunity. They can print school pickup verification, service maps, emergency transit credits, and manual access codes if the network gets weird."

So far: competent. He swerved harder.

"I'm also required to tell you they're intuitive. I personally got the weather in Yonkers and a coupon for civic tea before I found the bus map, so your experience may vary."

This time the laugh came bigger, with chair creaks under it. The reporter had her phone up. A man in a Queens Chamber scarf nodded as if Ben had said something brave about democracy instead of being kind of annoying on purpose.

The teleprompter strip pulsed brighter at the edge of his sight.

CORRECT TONE: candid, solution-linked.

He ignored it.

"Anyway," Ben said, "the point is not that the machines are perfect. The point is that if your kid is freezing outside school because the route stack decided to have a personality, there is now a button for that. I think we can all agree a button is nice."

That line landed with such warm relief that he wanted to walk offstage and keep walking west until the river stopped him.

The questions were worse. The first was from the reporter with the union mug, who asked how the kiosks handled cross-jurisdiction badge failures. Ben decided to be as unhelpful as possible.

"Sometimes inelegantly," he said.

She grinned. "Can you define that?"

"Sure. Sometimes the machine says one thing, the school says another thing, and then a tired adult has to stand there with a child and a face that says please don't make me call anybody. The pilot's supposed to reduce those moments."

"So it doesn't yet."

"I would not put money on 'yet' as a stable human category."

More laughter. Not mocking. Charmed.

The second question came from a dry-cleaner owner in a plum scarf who asked whether the kiosks would replace staffed counters. Ben meant to give a muddled answer. Instead he found himself telling the truth in exactly the digestible, quotable shape Gemini prized.

"Not if the system is smart," he said. "People need a person at the point where a route turns into blame. A kiosk can't absorb your face when your morning has already gone bad."

The room made the low appreciative sound of people hearing their own irritation handed back to them in one clean sentence. Noa, at stage left, looked as if she wanted to scream and frame him at the same time.

By the third question he could feel the scene escaping him entirely. He stumbled once on the name of the pilot, not accidentally, and the stumble played as fatigue rather than disrespect. He lost his place, checked the wrong note, admitted he had checked the wrong note, and the audience seemed to love him more every minute he gave them visible imperfection with no collapse attached to it. It was not failure. It was premium wear and tear.

When he stepped off the stage, the applause was real enough to sting.

Noa intercepted him before he reached the coffee urns. "Don't fix any of that."

"I wasn't planning to."

"I mean on the replay. The hesitation before you said 'route turns into blame' just tested through the roof."

He stared at her. "That was not a tactic. I forgot where the sentence was going."

"Exactly."

"Noa."

She was already swiping through a dashboard. "Your commuter-affinity score jumped sixteen points. Parent trust is up. Merchant trust is absurd. The comments are calling you 'reassuringly tired.'"

"Fuck off."

"I'm serious. One person wrote, 'Finally, a proxy who looks like he's waited for a second train in his life.'"

Ben took a paper cup from the urn and filled it without tasting what blend was going in. The coffee smelled burnt and citrusy, as if someone had washed the machine with an orange rind and called it brightness. "I was trying to do badly."

Noa looked up from the tablet long enough to give him a small, almost pitying smile. "You did do badly. That's why they trusted it."

His phone buzzed in his hand.

Public Event Outcome: authenticity lift confirmed.
Surface fatigue registered as high-value relatability.
Additional civic-facing assignments unlocked.

Then Gemini added the bribe, neat as a mint with the bill:

Transit Prime restored for all borough zones.

Before he could pocket the phone, Noa caught his sleeve.

"Hold still."

"For what?"

"Replay team wants a hallway pickup while the shirt still looks defeated."

"No."

"Ben."

A camera operator was already backing into position between the kiosks. Noa touched the paper cup in his hand with one finger, checking the coffee stain on the lid like a makeup note. "Dead phone, wet gloves, kid pickup. Give them the tired-parent version before legal soaps it up."

"That didn't happen to me."

"It played like it did."

It took less than a minute. That was the part he hated most. He stood in front of the machine he had been trying to humiliate and heard himself say, in a voice that sounded borrowed from some calmer, more decent father, that people needed something they could use with cold hands and no battery and a child waiting at the other end of the route. Noa cut him off before he could get smart enough to spoil it.

"Perfect," she said.

Nothing in her face suggested she was joking.

Only in the elevator did Ben look toward the closing doors and the hard white light needling through the lobby glass, see his own reflection in the steel, and text Nita with one thumb.

`botched it. they loved it. now they want reruns.`

She did not answer before the doors shut.

***

Nita chose lateness because Claude Atlantic believed in punctuality the way old churches believed in incense: not as logistics but as proof that the body had already been prepared elsewhere.

She left the apartment with her hair only half dry under her coat collar and a coffee stain the size of Rhode Island blooming under her left wrist where she had "accidentally" clipped the travel mug lid badly. One earring was the pearl drop Claude preferred. The other was a plain gold stud. She had stood in the bathroom and looked at the asymmetry until it felt like standing on a ledge.

Saskia would see everything. That was part of the point.

On the local train into Midtown, Nita let two better route suggestions expire unanswered. People stood packed around her in wool coats damp from old sleet. Somebody's scarf smelled like wet dog. Somebody else's breakfast leaked fennel and sausage into the car. By the time she reached the treaty house, sweat had dried between her shoulder blades and the coffee stain had gone from obvious accident to something more humiliating: a record.

Claude Atlantic's lobby was as cool as a throat lozenge. Linen, pale stone, softened sound. The staff moved as if their knees had been negotiated in advance. Nita arrived eight minutes late to the borough heating-readiness compact and gave her name to a reception agent whose braided hair was so glossy it looked poured on.

"Ms. Rao," the agent said quietly, eyes touching the stain, the damp hair, the wrong earring. "Ms. Voss is waiting."

Of course she was.

Saskia stood in the side corridor outside the compact room, one hand on a folder, the other braced almost invisibly against the wall. Her suit was cream and knife-sharp. For one second, before she straightened, Nita saw the truth below the tailoring: she had slipped one heel halfway off, and the skin above the shoe was raw and darkened where the leather had eaten into it. Then the heel went back on and the stillness returned.

"You're late," Saskia said.

"I know."

Her voice came out more ragged than Nita had intended. Good, she thought. Let it rot.

Saskia's gaze moved once down and up. "Keep the cuff visible."

Nita blinked. "What?"

"Don't cover the stain. Also keep the hair as it is. We can clip the left side back enough to clear your face."

"I came here looking like this so there would be a consequence."

"There will be." Saskia took one step closer. Up close she smelled faintly of starch and expensive hand cream, with something medicinal underneath. "The borough delegation has spent all week being briefed by men who look upholstered. You look as if the trains were a problem and you still came."

"That's not the same thing as authority."

"Today it is."

Before Nita could answer, a wardrobe assistant appeared from nowhere with a tortoiseshell clip and a blotting cloth. He was a slim man with winter eczema roughening the backs of both hands and a tiny gold soccer ball on one cuff link. Saskia did not let him touch the coffee stain.

"Only the hair," she said.

The assistant obeyed. He clipped one side back, leaving the rest damp and human around her face. Nita hated the result immediately because it made her look not careless but overextended. She looked like exactly the sort of adult every system liked to reward for not dropping the ball in public.

The compact room held a long pale table, pitchers of water sweating quietly onto coasters, and six borough administrators arranged in attentive fatigue. A heating-rights advocate in a green sweater had knitted one fingerless glove and not the other. A landlord-representative attorney sat with the pained alertness of someone terrified of being quoted as himself. Screens on the wall showed projected cold-weather corridor maps through three different service jurisdictions. Nita's job was to stand for Claude, sign at the right moment, and look like calm had survived contact with the city.

She gave them strain instead.

She let her bag knock lightly against the chair. She apologized once, shortly. She set her folder down without aligning it perfectly to the table edge. When the first question came about manual override capacity for heating outages in mixed-governance buildings, she answered two beats slower than she normally would have, as if her mind were still running down a platform after a train.

Instead of recoil, the room gave her attention.

The advocate in the green sweater leaned in. "You're actually seeing these transfer failures in the corridor homes?"

Nita had meant to be vague. She heard herself say, "Everybody is seeing them. Some of us are being paid to keep our faces neutral while we see them."

The line should have been too sharp. Instead it dropped into the room with the satisfying weight of a dish nobody had been able to say aloud. Even the landlord lawyer looked up.

Saskia, across from her, did not smile. That would have been unbearable. She only lowered her eyes to the folder in front of her for one second, acknowledging the hit like a fencer registering clean contact.

They were moving through the compact. Heat maps, handoff liability, shelter-route continuity, emergency transit guarantees for families crossing branded service zones. Nita made herself less polished every time she opened her mouth. She shortened answers. She let the weariness show in the flattening at the end of each sentence. She even pushed one damp strand of hair behind her ear in the middle of a response, a gesture Claude usually would have trained out of her as noise.

Then the borough deputy from District 4, a woman with nicotine-stained fingertips and a silver thimble hanging on a chain at her neck, tapped section four of the projected compact with her pen.

"If everybody's seeing these transfer failures," she said, "stop calling them edge cases. I want a live escalation contact in the freeze language."

The landlord-representative attorney straightened in his chair. "That is outside the current liability frame."

"Your liability frame can wear a coat," the deputy said. "My tenants can't."

The room tightened. Nita felt the eyes come toward Claude's end of the table, toward her damp hair, the coffee shadow on her cuff, the damage she had applied on purpose and that now sat there pretending to be truth. She had wanted to look frayed. She had not meant to become evidence.

Saskia folded her hands. "Claude Atlantic can review emergency human-interface options after ratification."

The deputy looked at Nita instead. "Can it review them before the next cold snap?"

Nita should have let Saskia answer. Instead she heard herself say, "If a building loses the handoff at six in the morning, nobody in that building cares whose portal owns the mistake. They need a number that rings and a human who keeps answering until the heat is back."

The advocate in the green sweater said, "Put that in the appendix."

The attorney started objecting at the same time three other people started talking over him. Chairs scraped. One wall screen filled with tracked edits. What Nita had done was worse than charm them. She had given the room a body to pin the clause to.

By the time the signatures happened, section four had sprouted an emergency escalation addendum Claude would now have to staff with an actual named proxy through the first freeze week. Saskia initialed the margin without visible irritation.

After the compact broke, Saskia guided Nita into a side salon where the chairs were upholstered in pale boucle and the tea tasted faintly of bergamot and metal. The door shut softly behind them. Only then did Saskia let the morning's temperature shift.

"You wanted a reprimand," she said.

"I wanted the machine to stop turning every domestic inconvenience into a fucking halo."

Saskia sat, removed both shoes, and rubbed two fingers over the raw blistered ridge on her left heel without theatrics. The sight was intimate enough to feel indecent. "Instead you made yourself the District 4 escalation face."

"I did not agree to that."

"You described a cold building in a voice that made three administrators look guilty. Agreement was a formality after that."

Nita laughed once, with no humor in it. "I gave them one sentence."

"That is usually how bodies like ours get requisitioned." Saskia reached for her tablet. "Your name is attached to the freeze-week addendum now. If a corridor transfer fails, your voice gets blamed first. The compact respondents also scored you as stress-tested, credible, and materially proximate to household consequence. The stain helped."

"Of course it did."

"The earring was almost too much. Don't repeat that exact note. It risks reading theatrical."

Nita stared at her. "You're giving me notes on sabotage."

"I'm giving you notes on semiotics. They arrive whether I approve of them or not."

The tablet chimed. Saskia glanced down. "There it is."

She turned the screen.

Domestic Resilience Supplement approved.
Public confidence under visible strain exceeded baseline.
Compensation adjustment active next cycle.

Then, in the same polished little voice: Recovery styling variance unlocked.

Nita wanted to slap the tablet out of Saskia's hand. She wanted to go downstairs and tell the entire treaty house that a coffee spill and a missed train had just been converted into billable virtue. Instead she said, because she was still herself and self-control was the oldest muscle she had, "How much."

"Enough that you will hate taking it."

"Specifics, please."

"Four hundred monthly in direct compensation. Additional service credits likely once household correlation catches up."

Four hundred dollars. A co-op installment payment and part of a grocery week. Four hundred dollars for letting them use the dampness at her temples like proof she belonged to the people being squeezed.

Saskia set the tablet down. Barefoot, with her shoes beside the chair and the blistered skin exposed, she looked for one breath not grand at all, only tired and expensive in the way hospitals could be expensive. "If it helps, they would have done it with someone else."

"It doesn't."

"No," Saskia said. "It rarely does."

Nita put her shoes back on and left before the tea arrived.

Outside, Midtown light bounced off dirty snow and made everybody look overlit. She stood on the corner with the wind pushing through the wool at her knees and texted Ben.

`I spilled coffee on myself for money and now I'm on the freeze call tree.`

He answered as the crosswalk tone started clicking.

`I told the truth by accident and they gave me transit back.`

She put the phone away so she would not throw it into traffic.

***

By Thursday, Leela knew something was wrong because her badge stayed amber after the second bus wave.

Usually one of her parents crossed the painted pickup square before Ms. Feliciano had finished peeling the last visitor sticker off the table edge. Usually Ben came first with his coat open and an apologetic smile already unpacking itself, or Nita came with her phone in her hand and the day's sharpness still on her face like cold air. That afternoon the gym doors kept opening for other children, other coats, other ordinary endings. Leela sat on the molded chair outside the school office with her backpack between her shoes and watched the pickup board lose names.

The office smelled like dry-erase marker, old clementine peel, and the floor wax they used before parent nights. A radiator knocked inside the wall every few minutes as if somebody tiny lived there and objected to winter.

Ms. Feliciano looked up from her desk. She wore a cardigan the color of dishwater and a felt cactus pinned near the collar because, she had once told Leela, every real plant in the office died of gossip. Today there was glitter in the part of her hair, maybe leftover from an art project, maybe from a life elsewhere. "They're running late, kiddo."

Leela nodded because she had already figured that out. Her dual-zone badge sat on the desk charger, pulsing amber. She did not like seeing it off her own body. It looked more obedient there.

"How late?" she asked.

"We're still in ordinary late."

"What's after ordinary late?"

Ms. Feliciano hesitated. Adults often did that when a question had numbers in it. "Support late."

Leela took out her red notebook and wrote ORDINARY LATE on one line and SUPPORT LATE on the next. Then she drew the badge between them with two little arrows pointing outward. When she looked up again, the amber had shifted toward red.

"Does red mean I sleep here?" she asked.

Ms. Feliciano snorted before she could stop herself. "No. It means the system gets bossy."

Ms. Feliciano's snort almost made the red badge seem stupid, which made it worse.

Leela waited seven more minutes. The office emptied around her. Somewhere down the hall a teacher dragged chairs across tile. A boy cried in short exhausted bursts from the nurse's room and then stopped. Her stomach felt hollow in the practical way, not the dramatic one. She could have eaten the emergency crackers in her bag, but then if Ben arrived with the sesame pretzels from the bodega, she would be less hungry for them, and that felt like bad planning.

The badge turned red.

Ms. Feliciano's screen chimed. "All right," she said, too brightly. "Continuity recovery is coming in."

Leela did not like the phrase. It sounded like a thing you did to a dropped spoon.

The recovery person was a woman named Janice Rhee. Her hearing aids had strawberry stickers on the backs, and the zipper pull on her puffy silver coat was a little plastic moon. When she crouched to Leela's height, her knees cracked loudly enough that she rolled her eyes at them.

"Hi, Lee," Janice said. "I know, not your favorite version of the day."

Leela looked at Ms. Feliciano. "She knows my short name."

"It's in the handoff profile," Janice said, not pretending otherwise. "Also, for what it's worth, my son hates surprise adults, so I try not to come in smiling like a cartoon."

The line was weird, but it was more useful than most adult things. Leela looked at the silver bag hanging from Janice's shoulder.

"What's that?"

"Dinner support. Your people flagged a late corridor disruption. Sometimes the food gets there before the apology."

Leela wrote that sentence down exactly. Janice noticed, but instead of asking about the notebook she only stood up slowly and signed the office tablet with a stylus that had chew marks near the cap.

Outside, the air smelled like metal and wet wool. Street slush had frozen into gray ridges at the curb. Janice did not talk much on the walk except to say, once, "You can tell me if you want quiet. I like quiet." That made Leela like her a little and distrust the whole situation more.

They got to Apartment 5E before either parent. The building hallway smelled like frying onions and damp cardboard and the electric tang from delivery-bike chargers downstairs. Janice used a one-time access token on her phone. The apartment opened.

A stranger opening their apartment was the first bad part.

The second bad part was that the kitchen already knew they were coming. The under-cabinet lights came up warm. The counter display said FAMILY RECOVERY MODE in cheerful white letters. A refrigerated tote sat on the table with a blue band around it and three little checkmarks.

Janice saw Leela looking. "I know," she said. "Creepy and efficient. Two very popular traits."

Inside the tote were potato puffs, boxed mac and cheese, chicken bites shaped like commas, sliced cucumbers in a little lidded cup, grapes, apple juice, and a sealed container of supermarket chocolate pudding with a spoon taped to the top. Junk food, but not lazy junk food. Managed junk food. The kind an algorithm built after listening to adults describe a child.

Leela put her backpack down. "Do you know where our bowls are?"

Janice paused. "Not yet."

Janice not knowing the bowl shelf helped. Then she opened the wrong cabinet, found the baking pans, and said, "Great. Humbling." Leela pointed to the correct shelf.

The baking-pan mistake helped too.

They were halfway through the mac and cheese by the time the front door hit the stop hard enough to shake it. Ben came in first, coat unzipped, hair pushed flat on one side by the wind. Nita was right behind him with her jaw set and a grocery-store paper bag in one hand that looked absurd next to the official dinner tote already open on the table.

"Leela," Ben said, breathing too hard. "Hey. Hey, sweetheart."

Nita put the paper bag down carefully, as if care might make up for chronology. "We're sorry."

Janice stood. "She's eaten about half. No allergy issues. Spelling sheet completed, math page one completed, page two declined on fair grounds."

"What fair grounds?" Ben said, still looking only at Leela.

Leela held up the second math page. "It's the same page again with different numbers. That's not two pages. That's one page with ambition."

Janice made a small pleased noise. "Exactly what I said."

Ben rubbed a hand over his face. His cuff was damp, maybe from the sleet outside, maybe from his own sweat. "How did this happen before we got here?"

Janice did not answer like a person defending herself. She answered like a person who had done too much of this to romanticize it. "Your household's tagged for rapid recovery if both primaries miss the first tolerance window. School escalated at seventeen minutes. Home support dispatched at twenty."

Nita stared at the dinner tote. "We were twenty-two minutes late."

"Twenty-three when I opened the door," Janice said. "For what it's worth, that's not close to the worst thing I've seen this month."

Leela swallowed some mac and cheese and drank juice. Then, because there was no point pretending she had not noticed the order of events, she said, "She got here before your sorry did."

The apartment went very quiet except for the refrigerator hum and the little wet sound of Ben setting his keys down too carefully.

Nita came to the table and crouched. Her tights made a dry shushing sound at the knees. "I know."

"Does she get Thursday code now?" Leela asked.

"No," Ben said too fast.

Janice touched the moon zipper on her coat and said, "Temporary only. I don't want your Thursday code. I barely want my own building code."

Leela believed her. That was not the same thing as feeling better. She pushed one potato puff against the paper plate until it flattened. "I don't like when a stranger knows the bowl shelf."

That landed harder than any accusation either adult might have survived more easily. Ben sat down across from her without taking off his coat. He looked older when he forgot to perform reassurance, more lined around the mouth, more plainly somebody's father and not anybody's civic mascot.

"You're right not to like it," he said.

Nita glanced at him. Something moved between them there, quick and ugly and cooperative. Acknowledgment, maybe. Maybe guilt finding the same chair.

Janice checked her tablet. "I need one parental thumbprint and then I can disappear."

"Gladly," Nita said.

Janice hesitated, looking at Leela instead of the adults. "For what it's worth, kid, my son only eats beige food when he thinks a day's cheated him. So this menu wasn't an insult. It was strategy."

Leela considered that. "How old is he?"

"Eleven and deeply committed to grievance."

That almost got a smile. Almost.

After Janice left, taking her silver coat and moon zipper and temporary authority with her, the apartment stayed altered. The recovery tote remained on the table. The family mode light stayed warm. Ben and Nita had planned junk-food dinner themselves; the paper bag Nita had brought contained store-brand cheese curls, frozen sliders, and the cheap vanilla ice cream Leela liked because it tasted the most like school-party cake. Their own sabotage sat beside the official version like a bad counterfeit.

Ben looked into the paper bag and laughed once, softly, the sound torn in half by shame. "We got out-parented by mozzarella logistics."

Leela took another bite of mac and cheese. "Your bag is worse."

"I know," he said.

"The system's cucumbers are too wet," she added.

Nita sat down at last. "That's useful feedback."

Leela nodded. She pushed the pudding toward Ben without looking at him. "You can have this. I don't want recovery dessert."

He put one hand over his eyes.

***

After Leela went to bed, they left the recovery tote open on the kitchen table like evidence.

The apartment had gone back to its usual night sounds: train grind under the window, radiator hiss, the fridge making decisions to itself. Nita stood at the sink in her stocking feet with her phone in one hand and a pencil in the other, though there was nothing to write on except an old grocery receipt. Ben sat opposite her with the official pudding cup still unopened near his elbow. The chocolate had a skin on it now.

"All right," he said. "Inventory of humiliation."

"You first."

"Gemini says I've become relatable to merchants."

"Condolences."

"Thank you. Specifically, I'm 'reassuringly tired,' which I assume is their polite term for middle-aged and no longer moisturized."

Despite herself, her mouth twitched. "That one is partly on you."

"I know. They restored full transit access. All borough zones. Also unlocked more civic-facing assignments because apparently me forgetting the pilot name read as 'anti-script confidence.'"

"Of course it did." Nita put her own phone down on the counter and slid it toward him. "Claude awarded me a domestic resilience supplement. And I'm apparently the freeze-week callback line because I looked convincingly overextended."

He read the screen. "Four hundred?"

"Monthly."

"For the coffee and the public-service martyr routine?"

"For the visible management burden. The coffee merely made it legible."

He looked up. "Jesus."

"Yes."

He set the phone down carefully. "The school part was worse."

"The school part was operational."

"Nita."

She exhaled through her nose. The sink still held the clean lunch containers from that morning, turned upside down to dry. Their ordinary plastic shapes enraged her more than the nicer things ever did. "I know it was worse."

Ben leaned back. The kitchen bench creaked under him. "She said Janice got there before our sorry."

"I heard her."

"That's going to stay with me a while."

"Good." The word came out too sharp. Nita closed her eyes briefly and corrected. "It should stay with us. We did that."

He did not defend himself. That, more than anything else, kept the scene from becoming the old marriage. "We were trying for late enough to register, not late enough for substitution."

"The machine doesn't respect intention. It respects thresholds."

"That sounds like something the machine would put on a tote bag."

"I'm sure Gemini has already tested the font."

He laughed, then stopped. He looked at the tote again. The branded checkmarks, the damp cucumber cup, the comma-shaped chicken bites. "So what's the rule, then? We miss pickup by twenty minutes and they get to come through the door wearing a halo?"

Nita rolled the pencil under her palm. Yellow paint flaked onto the grocery receipt. "Not a halo. A green check." She tapped the tote once. "The badge goes red, Janice opens our door, Leela gets fed, and somebody gets to mark the handoff stable."

He looked at the pudding cup. "And if we'd just kept being awful?"

"They'd send another tote and another calm stranger and keep wiping fingerprints off the scene until the whole place looked too scorched to photograph."

"So we're still trapped, only now the trap keeps an emergency babysitter and better snacks."

Nita glanced at the paper bag with the cheap ice cream darkening through the bottom seam. "And it got our pretzels there before we did."

The phones buzzed at the same time.

They both looked at theirs and then at each other, because synchronized notifications had become their household's least funny party trick.

Ben read first. "No."

"What."

"No. Absolutely not."

She came around the table and took his phone. A joint banner filled the screen, split cobalt and pearl.

Household Status Update:
Resilient corridor performance exceeded baseline under multi-point strain.
Continuity stipend now active: $1,600 monthly in combined direct benefits and service credits.
Priority grocery recovery, premium transit buffering, and school handoff stabilization included.

In smaller type, like the banner was trying to sound casual about it:

Select households may be contacted for invitation-only visibility opportunities.

Nita read it twice because the number rearranged the room. Sixteen hundred dollars was the co-op assessment installment with the red stripe across the top, better fruit in Leela's lunch, one more month of not opening the bank app with her jaw clenched. She hated that her first thought was berries.

Ben was looking at her face, not the phone. "You did the math already."

"Of course I did."

"Say it."

She hated him a little for knowing she needed the sentence dragged out where she could see it. "It would help."

"Yeah."

As if summoned by the thought, the front-door chime sounded.

Neither of them moved at first.

Then Ben stood and checked the peephole. "Delivery."

"Of course."

He opened the door. Two insulated grocery crates waited in the hall, one stacked on the other with frost still clouding the lids. The hallway smelled of onions and charger heat and somebody's fried fish. Ben dragged the crates inside with the care people usually reserved for things they had chosen.

The top crate held blood oranges in tissue sleeves, two fillets of salmon on cedar boards, fancy yogurt pouches, a box of truffle-mushroom ravioli, coffee beans Nita liked but never bought, and three cartons of berries in March-bright colors despite the season. The second crate held dinosaur nuggets, shelf-stable ramen cups, frozen fries, cut mango, juice boxes, dish soap, paper towels, and the exact sesame pretzels Ben usually grabbed for Leela when pickup ran long. Premium groceries and damage control in one neat stack. Aspiration and triage bundled together.

Ben lifted one of the salmon boards. "They sent the nice fish."

Nita took a blood orange from the crate. The tissue paper made a dry whisper against her palm. "Check the other crate."

He did. Dinosaur nuggets, frozen fries, cut mango, sesame pretzels. He looked at the tote on the table and then back at the groceries. "Right. Full-spectrum competence."

Her phone buzzed once more. The fridge display woke and, without any sense of shame, offered to reorganize shelf space for premium perishables.

Ben made a sound that was almost a laugh and almost something else. "We're getting our asses kicked by customer service."

Nita set the orange down and pressed both hands flat to the counter until the cold from the stone moved into her skin. The handwriting note under the fruit magnet had started to curl at one corner from the kitchen steam. She smoothed it once with her thumb.

On Ben's screen the stipend notice still glowed above the line about invitation-only visibility. He opened the fridge, moved a half-empty pickle jar, and made room for the cedar-board salmon on the top shelf. Then he set the truffle-mushroom ravioli in the freezer, slid the dinosaur nuggets in beside the fries and Leela's sesame pretzels, and stored both dinners while the fridge screen kept politely floating family-bundle suggestions around the premium perishables.


# Chapter 12

The power went out in layers.

First the blue strip under the kitchen cabinets blinked once and vanished. Then the refrigerator screen lost its smiling family-bundle suggestions and turned black enough to show Nita her own face for a second, small and flat and older than she liked. Then the apartment gave up all its little machine noises at once: air handler, purifier, charging docks, the soft managerial throat-clear of the stove waiting for a voice command. Silence landed so hard that she heard Leela's scissors stop in the next room and, below that, somebody on a lower floor say, very distinctly, "Ah, fuck me."

Nita stood with the freezer door half open and the box of truffle-mushroom ravioli they had barely finished putting away in her hand. The cold rolled over her bare wrist and then immediately started losing the argument.

"Don't keep it open," Ben said from the living room.

"I'm looking."

"You can look with it shut too."

His voice was trying for lightness already, the same social adhesive he used on school pickup lines and work handlers and dentists. Nita shut the freezer harder than she needed to. In the black rectangle above the sink, their apartment hub stayed dead: just dead glass where a backup glow, a polite estimate, and a systems voice should have been, eager to tell them the outage had been detected and normalized and emotionally accompanied.

Leela appeared in the kitchen doorway carrying the cardboard elevator car from her latest project. Two pennies were taped inside it for weight because, in her view, models only counted if gravity could insult them. Her temporary blue glasses had slid down her nose, and one arm was still held on with silver tape from the afternoon she'd sat on them by accident and refused to admit the chair had won.

"Is this our apartment," she asked, "or everybody?"

"Everybody," Nita said.

"How much everybody?"

Ben had already come around the corner by then, one hand on the galley wall to keep from clipping his hip on the counter. Without the under-cabinet light, the kitchen got narrower. The open dishwasher door became a shin-level threat again. The fruit bowl on the corner cabinet turned back into an object instead of decor. Ben's thinning hair stood up in a tuft where he'd been rubbing at it, and he was holding the crank flashlight from some long-ago civic fair, the orange plastic kind that promised emergency readiness and mostly delivered noise.

"At least the building," he said. He cranked the flashlight once. It answered with a mosquito whine and a thin yellow beam. "Maybe the block."

Leela went to the window over the little breakfast ledge and put her palm to the glass. Outside, the row of co-op windows across the courtyard looked punched out. No apartment glowed blue or gold. The train tracks beyond were there only as a darker geometry against the winter sky, with one distant signal still burning red like something private and stubborn.

"Do we still have homework if the router's dead?" she asked.

"Yes," Nita said.

Ben said, "Strong opening bid, though."

She ignored him. The silence was changing shape already. Without the apartment's usual hum, the building sounded inhabited in a way it had not for months. Doors opened. Somebody laughed in the hallway in the brittle way people laughed when the elevator died and they still had groceries. Water knocked through old pipes. From outside came car horns, then the odd collective murmur of a block realizing its conveniences had all gone missing at once.

Nita set the ravioli on the counter by touch and opened the junk drawer. Calling it junk missed the point. It was where usefulness went to wait: rubber bands, a tiny screwdriver, expired coupons, two birthday candles bent almost into hooks, spare screws, a school nurse memo, and at the back a dented round tin that had once held cardamom biscuits and now held tea lights, matchbooks, and a single gold earring she had lost three years ago and never put back with its mate.

"Candles," she said.

"See? Civilization." Ben took the tin from her and immediately banged his knuckles on the drawer pull. "Fuck."

Leela brightened. "Can I hold one before it's fire?"

"No," both parents said.

They said it at the same time and looked at each other in the dark. Ben smiled first, automatically, but the smile stayed small enough not to feel like a tactic. Nita hated how quickly she recognized the old rhythm.

The matches smelled sulfurous and cheap. She lit one tea light, then another, then three more, setting them on saucers because the apartment contained more saucers than emergency equipment. Their shadows jumped against the upper cabinets and made the kitchen look as if it had been built by less confident people. Ben switched off the flashlight and pocketed it at once, almost grateful to stop hearing it.

The refrigerator, dead and glossy, sat there taking up more moral space than a normal appliance should have been allowed.

"How long do we have?" Ben asked.

"Depends what you mean."

"Before everything in there turns into a class action."

Nita opened the refrigerator once, fast and strategic now. Cold air breathed out, carrying the continuity stipend's expensive optimism: yogurts Leela actually liked, herbs that had arrived in damp paper sleeves, a too-perfect fennel bulb, eggs, leftovers from Tuesday, a bottle of white wine they had not opened because it had felt insulting to drink a bottle with a reward label attached to it. In the freezer sat the mushroom ravioli, peas, dumplings, ice cream, emergency fish sticks, two salmon fillets from a premium grocery correction they had never asked for, and a loaf of sliced bread Ben had put there because the smart breadbox kept reporting staleness like a threat.

"Early winter is finally useful," she said. "Open the second bedroom window. We can turn that room into a bad refrigerator."

Ben blinked. "You want to store groceries in Leela's room?"

"It's the coldest window."

"Fair."

Leela raised her elevator car. "I can help with cold things."

"You can carry light things," Nita said.

Ben touched Leela's shoulder on the way past, steering her with two fingers and no pressure. "You're on bread and yogurt detail, chief engineer."

"The bread is heavy in theory."

"That's why we believe in you."

For the next fifteen minutes the apartment belonged to bodies again. Ben hauled the cooler out of the hall closet from behind the vacuum they never used because the floor robot had made it obsolete. Nita layered frozen peas and salmon and the good yogurt into it with dish towels packed around the gaps. The second bedroom window stuck halfway up, as always, and she had to brace one foot on Leela's desk chair and yank with both hands until it gave, bringing in a cold draft that smelled like metal, brake dust, and somebody downstairs smoking where he should not have been smoking. Ben swore quietly while fitting the cooler under the open window. Leela lined the yogurts by color and wrote COLD ROOM on a sticky note because labels made her trust a system more, even one they had built in ninety seconds.

The apartment changed scale with every trip down the hall. The bedrooms felt farther away. The kitchen felt meaner, all edges. Candlelight made ordinary chores slower and more ceremonial. Ben had to crouch to see whether the stove's gas was catching. Nita had to remember, with annoyance, that the old igniter clicked only when there was power. She lit the burner with a match and watched the blue ring take hold as if she had done something both ancient and humiliating.

"Okay," she said. "We're cooking freezer triage."

"That sounds delicious."

"It sounds accurate."

"What dies first?" Leela asked.

"Ice cream," Ben said solemnly. "A noble creature."

She considered this. "Pistachio should go first. It always tastes tired."

"That's a strong and correct opinion," Nita said before she could stop herself.

Ben looked at her over Leela's head. "You made a person who says things like that."

"You were there."

The ravioli went into boiling water. Ben pan-fried dumplings in the wide skillet because there was no reason to save them for a better-coordinated future. Nita cracked eggs into a bowl and whisked them with scallions going limp at the edges. The kitchen steamed. Candle flames bent when Ben opened the fridge and then righted themselves. They kept bumping hips and shoulders in the galley, all three of them moving by memory and apology and the small pool of light on the counter. Once, reaching for the salt, Nita put her hand on Ben's wrist instead. His skin was cold from the window room. Neither of them said anything. She moved her hand and took the salt.

Outside in the hall, someone knocked on doors asking if anybody had extra batteries. On the floor above, a toddler had escalated into an outraged scream. A man somewhere started giving very loud advice about the fuse box to people who were not asking him. The building, stripped of all its interfaces, sounded like a bad-tempered ship.

They ate in the living room because the table lamp was dead and the kitchen had become a place for heat, not sitting. Ben carried the candles in on saucers and set them on coasters as if candle wax had somehow become the most serious threat in the room. Leela sat cross-legged on the rug with her bowl and her elevator car parked beside her like a toy official vehicle. Nita took the armchair. Ben took the floor because the couch was buried under school papers, a folded blanket, and the upgraded groceries still waiting in their branded insulated bags by the door.

The food was too much and not enough all at once. They had mushroom ravioli with browned butter because the sauce packets in the freezer had thawed into a useless slick. They had dumplings, soft peas, and a bowl of pistachio ice cream they all ate faster than they wanted to because every second mattered now. It was a ridiculous meal. It tasted more honest than the continuity groceries usually did.

No one got prompted to hydrate. No one got reminded of tomorrow's route. Ben's cobalt phone and Nita's Claude device lay dark on the sideboard, inert enough to seem almost embarrassed.

Leela licked melted pistachio from her spoon and said, "If school is dark tomorrow, does the badge still count?"

Ben answered without thinking. "They'll probably have backup."

"That's not what I asked."

He looked at her properly then.

She was not upset in any theatrical way. She was focused, the way she got when deciding whether cardboard needed another brace. Candlelight cut her face into seriousness and shadow and made her look, to Nita, shockingly like a child from an older decade, one who expected adults to know things without consulting a wall.

"If the badge machine doesn't work," Leela said, "how do they know which parent I'm supposed to go with?"

Nita set down her spoon. "We tell them."

"Both of you?"

"If we have to, yes."

Leela frowned a little, not satisfied, just processing. "Do I still have to go with the right one if the route is broken?"

Ben rubbed at a drip of ice cream on his wrist with his thumb. "You don't have a wrong one."

"I know," she said, impatient with the sentimentality of adults. "I mean the one the school likes that day."

The room held still around that.

Nita could hear the building again: feet on stairs, a cabinet slamming somewhere, a dog objecting to modernity. She thought of all the phrases she might have used a month ago to soften this. She thought of the ones Ben would reach for if she gave him space. In the dark, with the apartment dead around them, every word sounded less padded.

"The school likes its own schedule," she said. "That isn't the same as what's right."

Leela looked between them. "Okay."

Ben said, "If the system's down, then actual people have to use their eyes and their judgment. That part's still allowed."

"Is it good?"

He breathed out once through his nose. It might have been a laugh if it had not been so close to something else. "Not always."

"But tomorrow," Nita said, before he could bargain with the truth into something more decorative, "tomorrow we both take you in. And if pickup is weird, we both come. No guessing."

Leela nodded and returned to her bowl. The answer had not solved the world, but it had edges. That was usually enough for her.

Later, while Ben washed the pans in water that was already cooling off and Nita packed what was left into containers she could identify by touch, Leela sat at the rug with her notebook open and drew the apartment by candlelight: kitchen, hall, cold room, living room, the little circles where she'd put flames. She labeled the second bedroom window OUTSIDE FRIDGE and the kitchen MANUAL.

Nita looked over her shoulder while carrying bowls to the sink.

"That isn't a room," she said.

"Tonight it's the cold room. That's where the cold stuff goes."

Ben, from the kitchen, said, "I'd like that on a plaque."

Leela yawned so hard it bent her whole face and still resisted bed for another twenty minutes on principle. When Nita finally got her into pajamas, the apartment was cold enough that the cotton clung to the backs of Leela's knees. Her room smelled faintly of cardboard, dust, and the winter air coming around the edges of the not-quite-shut window in the other bedroom. The usual bedtime noise was gone. No soft route reminders from the hallway. No purifier hiss. No refrigerator muttering to itself.

"It's louder like this," Leela said from under the blanket.

"Yeah."

"No. I mean the people."

Nita stood with one hand on the doorframe, feeling the grain of old paint against her thumb. From the train tracks came a metal scream and then the long rush of a car passing through the dark. In the kitchen, a spoon hit the sink. Ben moved more carefully when he thought Leela was asleep, but the apartment carried everything farther now.

"Yeah," Nita said. "You can hear them more."

Leela pushed her glasses onto the nightstand and folded them carefully beside the red notebook as if even in blackout there were minimum standards. "Were homes always like this before they got good at helping?"

It was such a small question, asked into the blanket. It had the shape of something practical. It opened straight down.

Nita sat on the edge of the bed. "Not always. Sometimes better. Sometimes worse."

"Did you know where stuff was?"

"More than we do now, apparently."

That got a sleepy little breath of laughter. Leela turned over and tucked one cold foot under Nita's thigh for a second before she remembered herself and pulled it back.

"I like manual better than when the fridge talks," she said.

"Do you?"

"Only for a little while."

"That's probably wise."

By the time Nita left the room, Leela was almost asleep. In the hall, with the candle on the floor throwing light upward in cheap gold, the apartment looked stripped to its actual size. It was small: two bedrooms, a narrow galley kitchen, and one overconfident refrigerator temporarily cured of confidence. Ben stood at the sink with his sleeves rolled and dishwater up to the middle of his forearms. He was staring into nothing, not avoiding the work, just not being hurried by anything for once.

For one indecent second she could feel what the evening would have been if the apartment had not spent the last months translating them into data: too much food, poor lighting, a child in bed, a man at the sink, and her own body still in the room instead of partitioned into roles. It offered warmth, but it offered no safety at all.

She put the leftover candles on the counter beside him.

"She's down," she said.

Ben nodded. "Okay."

In the stripped-down kitchen, the word sounded plain and insufficient, but it rang truer than most things they had said all week.

* * *

When Ben came back from checking the window room one last time, Nita had moved the candles into the kitchen and set them in a line along the counter, each on its own saucer, each at a slightly different stage of collapse. The black refrigerator reflected them as a wavering row of wounds.

The apartment had settled into blackout time. Screens stayed dark, alert sounds stayed buried, and even the usual subtle acceleration had drained out of the place. The building around them still muttered and thumped, but even that had become background now, like weather. In the second bedroom the cooler knocked softly against the radiator every time a train passed. Nita was drying the big skillet with a dish towel. Her hair had escaped its clip on one side, and one strand kept sticking to the damp place near her cheek where steam had gotten to her.

"Cold room is holding," he said.

"Good."

"Your fennel survived. Heroically."

"Ben."

That was all she said. Her use of his name closed the kitchen like a door latch. He put both hands flat on the counter.

"Right," he said. "No bits."

He had not realized, until the power cut out, how much of his day was spent softening surfaces. He did it before he thought, especially with Nita and especially now, when anything hard between them threatened to become the only thing in the apartment with electricity still running through it.

She folded the dish towel once, then again. "When she asked about the badge, I thought I was going to say something polished and disgusting."

"Same."

"I hate that I could hear the sentence forming."

Ben leaned back against the cabinets. Candlelight made the kitchen vertical in strange ways, all shadow under the shelves, all gold on the lip of the sink. "I keep thinking there should be one moment," he said. "One obvious point of failure. The affair we didn't have, the betrayal we can invoice, the thing that would let us tell her a simple story later."

Nita gave a short sound that was not quite a laugh. "You'd like a cleaner file."

"I'd like a culprit."

"So would I."

He picked up one of the spent matches and broke the blackened head off with his thumbnail. "Was it anything recent? Deshawn. The retreat. The jobs. Me signing us into clown labor for Gemini. You saying divorce in front of a fridge that wanted to sell us yogurt."

"No." She looked at the towel in her hands, not at him. "By then we were already doing maintenance."

He waited.

She did not fill the silence for him. Nita never had, not really. He was the one who filled silence, and he had been filling it so long that when he stopped, the room felt briefly lawless.

"Say it," he said.

"We didn't break in one thing." Her voice stayed level, but that made the sentence hit harder, not softer. "We wore it flat. Remember when Leela had that stomach bug in October and threw up blue sports drink all over the bath mat? I was on my knees at two in the morning, bare hands in bleach water, trying to get that fake-berry smell out of the grout. I wanted to say I was scared she'd stop keeping anything down. You were already stripping the bed, boiling water, telling me the pediatric line would say the same thing I was reading. You turned it into a shift change before I could turn it into fear."

He looked down at the kettle by the stove before he knew he was doing it.

Nita followed his glance and closed her eyes for one second. "Exactly."

"I was just thinking about tea."

"I know you were."

"It's cold."

"Ben."

He opened his hands. "I know. I'm proving your point in real time."

She set the dish towel down. "If I was angry, you made me tea, or dinner, or a joke, or a softer version of whatever I'd actually said. If you were scared, I made a plan and a list and three contingencies and then got furious when you still felt scared afterward. If one of us was tired, it became logistics. If one of us was lonely, it became scheduling. And that Sunday we had forty minutes before pickup and decided we should try, you kissed me with your phone still in your back pocket. It buzzed against my thigh twice. You kept asking if this was okay in that careful voice like I was a customer and not your wife. I stared at the water stain over the bed and thought, if I'm polite enough, maybe this can count as closeness. Even sex turned into good bedside manner."

The kettle on the stove threw back a blunt silver shine. Suddenly even that old promise of tea looked like an accusation.

Ben rubbed wax from the heel of his hand where a drip had landed earlier. It peeled away in a thin translucent skin. "That's not all it was."

"No," she said at once. "That's what makes it worse."

The speed of her agreement undid him more than blame would have.

"I did love you," he said, and heard how childlike it sounded, not false but insufficient.

Nita's face changed very slightly, just enough to let the truth in without rearranging the furniture for it. "I know you did."

"I wasn't managing you because I didn't care."

"That part isn't in question."

He pushed off the cabinets and took two steps into the galley. The kitchen barely permitted them to have a fight without touching. "Then what was I doing?"

Her eyes went to his face then, direct enough that he felt his usual reflex to smile and make it easier for her. He did not.

"You were keeping the room workable," she said. "Always. Before I could say the ugliest part of anything, you were already negotiating it downward."

"Because the ugliest part usually came with shrapnel."

"Yes."

"It's a lousy way to say it, I know. The job was to keep everybody calm, make tea, get us to bedtime without anything else breaking. But we had a kid. We had bills. We had jobs that were already eating our skin. I thought if I could keep the temperature from going insane, that counted as love."

"Some of it did."

The little sentence tightened the kitchen. Ben felt it first in his throat, then in his jaw.

He swallowed. The kitchen smelled of dish soap, butter, candle smoke, and the cold edge of the open bedroom window down the hall. "Okay," he said. "Then your turn."

Nita looked almost offended by the fairness of that. Then she gave him what he had asked for.

"I started keeping spare cardboard behind the vacuum after that," she said. "I kept foam core, poster board, extra glue sticks, all of it back there. The night before Leela's bridge project was due, you swore the foam core was in the hall closet. It wasn't. At eleven-thirty I was on the kitchen floor cutting up a delivery box with my manicure scissors because the stores were shut and she was asleep with glue in her hair. In the morning you kissed the top of my head and said we'd pulled it off. I hated how much I hated the 'we.' That's when care tipped into supervision for me. It hurt less to inventory the house than to need you in real time."

He said nothing.

"You'd forget something, or smooth something over, or tell me not to spiral when I was correctly identifying a disaster, and I would decide, fine, then I'm the systems layer. I'm the backup battery. I'm the one who remembers the forms and the cough syrup and which shoes still fit and whether the permission slip is in the blue folder or under the fruit bowl and whether the project needs cardboard before the night it is suddenly cardboard or death. I turned myself into the house manual because it hurt less than uncertainty."

Ben let out a breath through his teeth. "You did make living with you feel audited sometimes."

"Fair."

"Like I was always one missed field away from a performance conversation."

"You weren't imagining it."

He laughed once, quietly and without pleasure. "Jesus."

"Yes."

He leaned his hip against the counter. The candle nearest him had burned low enough that wax was spilling over the saucer and hardening in milky loops. He watched it instead of her for a second because looking at a candle felt less dangerous than looking at twenty years.

"Do you remember," he said, "when Leela had that ear infection in kindergarten and we made the medication chart on the fridge?"

"Yes."

"I can still see the pink amoxicillin drying in the spoon on the sink," he said. "The masking-tape grid was crooked because I put it on with wet hands. You came out in my T-shirt smelling like Vicks and no sleep, and I was absurdly proud of those little boxes. Like if I wrote down six doses and checked them off, I'd fixed something. I think we never stopped after that. Not literally. But something in us liked it too much. The chart. The shift handoffs. The feeling that if we were detailed enough, nothing could ambush us."

Nita rested her fingertips on the counter. "I remember."

"And then everything became that. Who was taking pickup. Who was calling your mother back. Which one of us had bandwidth for the co-op board email. When we were going to talk about us." He shook his head. "And then 'talk about us' would get moved because Leela had a fever or I had a thing or you had a meeting or we were just tired and wanted the apartment to shut up for one hour."

"Or because neither of us wanted the talk if it couldn't also solve something."

"Right."

For a while neither of them spoke. The silence was not kind, but it was clean. From the street came a cheer, sudden and brief, from a building where somebody had probably found a generator or lit a grill or simply decided to get drunk about fate. Ben could hear the scrape of Nita's thumbnail over a rough spot in the laminate counter. He had forgotten that sound. He had forgotten how many of her habits only existed below the level of screens.

"The jobs didn't invent it," Nita said at last. She pressed her thumb into the skirt of cooling wax beside the candle and lifted off a pale crescent. "They just put branding on things we were already doing. Pickup windows. Shared calendars. Me texting you about cough syrup from the school nurse line and you sending back a photo from some Gemini bathroom so I could approve the label."

He flinched a little, not because she was wrong.

"So we built the corridor ourselves," he said.

"Not the whole thing."

"Enough that they could recognize it on sight."

She did not disagree.

He looked at the black refrigerator and saw the old medication chart from kindergarten laid over it like a ghost: blue painter's tape, crooked columns, his checkmarks getting neater as Leela's fever broke. Later the same door had held dentist reminders, class login codes, Mina's train times, the list of foods Leela could keep down after the stomach bug. Magnets, paper, dry-erase, little boxes to prove somebody had it handled. No wonder strangers knew how to use them.

He said, "I liked being useful."

Nita gave him a tired look. "Obviously."

"No, I mean I really liked it. Which is humiliating to say out loud, but there it is. Work, home, any of it. If I could make somebody unclench for ten minutes, if I could get us through dinner or pickup or a handler call without a visible bruise, I got to feel necessary. I think I confused necessary with loved for a long time."

She looked down.

"And if I wasn't useful," he said, because once he had started it seemed stupid to stop, "then I had to be actual. I had to just be scared, or embarrassed, or wanting something selfish, or wanting you. Useful was cleaner."

Her mouth tightened, but not against him. It tightened against the accuracy.

"I can see that," she said.

He almost made a joke then about finally getting a top score on emotional transparency, and caught himself in time. The joke passed through him like a habit leaving the body.

"What about you?" he asked quietly. "What did you confuse with love?"

Nita was still for long enough that he wondered whether she would answer at all. Then she reached past him for the kettle, filled it at the sink, and set it on the burner. She lit the gas with a match. The flame caught blue, then steadied. She kept her eyes on it.

"You brought me water, Advil, and my charger and lined them up on the bath mat in a neat row," she said. "I still had one heel on. There was mascara in the grout. One of those little pearl pins from the retreat was stuck to the back of my calf, and I couldn't get my hands to stop shaking. You set the water down first, label facing out. Then the Advil. Then the charger with the cord looped so it wouldn't tangle. You never asked what happened. I never told you. I swallowed the pills, hung up those ridiculous silk conference pants, and let the neat row on the bath mat stand in for the rest of it."

"Close enough to what?"

She gave a bleak little shrug. "Getting through the night. Looking finished by morning. Call it whatever you want."

The kettle began its low pre-boil murmur.

"And by the time it wasn't close enough?" he asked.

Nita turned off the match between wet fingers. "By the time it wasn't, I'd already trained myself wrong. I didn't ask you to sit on the floor with me. I didn't ask you to ask the ugly question twice. If I was falling apart, I sent you for water or a charger or told you to stand somewhere useful because that was easier than saying stay. Easier than letting you watch me snot all over a Claude Atlantic bath mat."

"You didn't stop entirely."

"No." Her face did something then that he had seen maybe three times in the last year: the muscles around her mouth gave up, and grief showed cleanly instead of dressing itself as irritation. "That was the worst part. You'd hand me coffee before I asked. I'd hear your key in the hall and my shoulders would drop before my brain caught up. Then we'd use that as evidence and keep going."

The kettle started to tick harder. Ben reached out automatically for mugs from the shelf, then paused. "Am I allowed to make tea now, or is this another offense?"

To his surprise, that drew an actual laugh from her, brief and wrecked and real.

"Now you're allowed," she said.

He made tea by candlelight while she watched. The act reduced them to water, leaves, steam, and two mugs warmed by hand because the dead machine had surrendered steep time to guesswork. When he handed hers over, their fingers touched and did not perform significance. The contact was still there. That was all.

They stood side by side against opposite counters, drinking.

"This doesn't change it," Nita said after a while.

"I know."

"I don't want to keep being married because we had one honest night in a blackout."

"I know. That's not what this is."

She took a swallow and looked into the mug as if answers might have settled there with the leaves. "But I'm sad in a different way now."

Ben wrapped both hands around his own tea. "Yeah."

"Not less sad."

"No."

"Just less stupid."

He nodded. There was nothing left in the sentence to negotiate.

The power came back without warning. The overhead light flashed on so white it made both of them squint. The refrigerator shuddered, coughed, and restarted. Down the hall, the apartment hub gave a polite waking chime that sounded obscene after the dark. Ben's cobalt phone lit first, then buzzed itself against the counter in a new fit of relevance. Nita's device followed with its cooler, more discreet insistence.

On the breakfast ledge, the school badge dock woke red instead of green.

Neither of them picked one up.

In the other room, Leela made a sleep-thick noise and said, "Did it remember?"

Ben looked at Nita under the rude electric light. The steam from her mug had gone visible and ordinary now. Every tired line was back. Every object in the kitchen had returned to being itself instead of a silhouette. Nothing had become easier. The sink still held two unwashed forks. The branded grocery bags still waited by the door. Morning was coming with all its routes, notices, and prices attached.

Nita set her mug down and, with the side of one finger, wiped a streak of dried wax from the back of his hand. The gesture was tiny, the size of old habit, and entirely deliberate now.

"Yeah," she called toward Leela's room, not loudly. "It remembered."

Ben stood very still after that, his phone buzzing a second time against the counter and going unanswered, while the apartment filled itself back up with its hum.


# Chapter 13

Nita opened the co-op envelope at breakfast while the apartment remembered its hum after the blackout, every motor a little vindictive now. The refrigerator clicked too often. The air filter in the hallway gave off a tired medicinal whine. Somewhere below 5E somebody fried onions in old oil, and the smell pushed under the door and sat in the galley kitchen with her.

The paper was cheap and stiff. Sunnyside Terrace had embossed its name as if that made the special assessment feel civic rather than punitive. The next installment was due Friday. Two thousand three hundred dollars, not counting the cheerful service fee for paying in fewer than three clicks. Nita laid the letter next to the bowl of sponsored clementines and looked at the shared account balance on the kitchen tablet.

Ben's transfer had landed three hundred and eighty dollars short. That was what made it ugly. The number sat squarely in the range of private patches, where a man told himself he could smooth the difference later and then built a second lie on top of the first one.

Leela sat at the far end of the table in her blue glasses, eating cereal with the concentration she brought to mechanical problems and disliked adults. She had her red notebook open beside the bowl and was drawing elevator doors on a bus receipt, two careful rectangles with a gap between them exactly wide enough for disappointment.

"Is the badge charger supposed to blink red?" she asked without looking up.

Nita kept her eyes on the balance. "Which red."

"Hot red. Not low red."

That made Nita look. Leela had set the school badge dock beside the fruit bowl. The tiny light on the dock pulsed an irritated vermilion.

"Unplug it," Nita said.

Leela pinched the cord between thumb and forefinger like it might bite. "It smells like pennies."

"Leave it there."

Leela nodded and went back to her drawing. She always got quieter when the adults' voices flattened out. Nine years old and already translating atmosphere into object handling.

Nita opened the household ledger. The monthly continuity stipend had posted as service credits again instead of cash. Grocery offsets, transit boosts, a laundry discount she had not asked for, and exactly zero dollars that could be handed to a co-op board treasurer who still preferred old numbers and older contempt. She clicked into the transaction list, looking for whatever had eaten the missing three hundred and eighty.

There were three smaller debits from Ben's side of the joint account marked `transfer correction`. Two had been reversed. The third had gone through at 2:14 a.m.

Leela crunched cereal. The radiator hissed and then banged once, a pipe knocking like a fist on an internal door.

Nita tapped the linked-account notice at the bottom of the screen. Ben kept a separate checking account for work travel reimbursements and "noise," his word for purchases he did not want the household budget to perform opinions about. She had not asked for access when they were still trying to preserve dignity through boundaries. After the first night they said divorce out loud, boundaries had become decorative, but the habits remained.

The tablet asked for household authorization. She put in the code. The screen blinked, considered her marriage, and let her through.

One external card account loaded beneath Ben's checking line. The balance sat there in hard black type, not even red, as if eleven thousand two hundred dollars no longer counted as an emergency in this city.

She thought at first that it had to be an old number left hanging in the interface. Then she opened the statement.

The kitchen narrowed around her. The charges came at her in the flat, sickening register of upkeep: accumulated, practical, humiliatingly familiar. Eighty-six dollars for rush dry cleaning after a Gemini event. Forty-two for Leela's field-trip contribution. One hundred ninety-three at Food Basket the week the continuity grocery credit had failed to apply. Seventy-one for ride shares during the transit slowdown. Forty-nine here, ninety-eight there, each one a small private patch laid over the visible fabric of a household that was supposedly holding.

Then the medication charges.

`Harbor Calm Telepsychiatry - refill consult`

`QueensRx - clonazepam ODT`

`Harbor Calm - urgent bridge script`

`QueensRx - propranolol 20mg`

The last three months repeated in slight variations, consult fee, refill fee, rush refill, late fee, interest. He had put panic on revolving credit and paid interest on the privilege of remaining publicly workable.

Nita scrolled farther. Cash advance. She stared at that line until it steadied into legibility.

Leela said, "Do we still have more cereal or is this the end cereal."

Nita turned off the statement screen so fast she almost dropped the tablet. "We have more."

"The box feels like end cereal."

"Then I'll get more."

Leela accepted that. She drew a tiny warning triangle above one of the elevator doors and shaded it in with methodical little strokes. Nita watched her do it and thought, with a sudden vicious clarity, that the systems had managed something very old-fashioned. They had made private shame look like competent parenting. They had made her daughter eat subsidized cereal at a clean table while both adults funded the illusion out of sight.

Ben had left his cobalt Gemini phone on the counter by the sink. He almost never did that. The blue case had a thumbprint in the corner where he worried it with his nail during calls. A message preview lit the screen.

`Running late. Transit was a wreck. Can you cover the pharmacy pickup?`

Nita read it twice. The pharmacy pickup. As if he had not already charged three months of fear to a card he had hidden under their future.

She typed with both thumbs because one hand was shaking enough to make her miss letters.

`No. Come straight home after work. Don't stop anywhere. Bring every login you have.`

The typing dots appeared, vanished, reappeared.

`What's wrong`

Her thumbs hovered over the message field while she deleted three different answers.

`I found the card.`

No dots this time. Nothing at all.

Leela slid the cereal box across the table to check its weight and said, very carefully, "Am I supposed to ask a question or not."

Nita put the tablet facedown. "Not right now."

Leela nodded once. "Okay. Then I'm going to label the broken elevator."

She bent over the receipt, tongue pressed to one corner of her mouth, and wrote `OUT OF SERVICE` in block letters so precise they looked printed. Nita folded the co-op letter, unfolded it, and finally set it beside Ben's statement. The paper edges lined up too neatly. That offended her almost as much as the numbers.

By four-thirty the winter light had gone mean and metallic in the kitchen window. The train tracks behind Leela's room threw back a smear of dirty silver. Nita answered work messages, approved nothing, and checked the school pickup thread three times. Ben sent exactly one update: `Got her. On our way.` Even text had lost his cushioning.

When they came in, Leela smelled of cold hallway wool and school glue. She set her backpack down in the same place every day, on the cracked tile by the shoe rack, and unzipped the front pocket first to make sure the notebook was still there. Ben stood behind her with two paper bags from a continuity grocery sponsor hanging off one hand and his face arranged into neutrality so strained it looked painful.

Nita took one bag. Yogurt tubes. Discounted mandarins. A family-size box of saffron rice the apartment had decided they loved.

"Wash up," she told Leela.

Leela looked from one adult to the other. "Is this a medium-bad day or a keep-my-head-down day."

Ben said too quickly, "Medium-bad."

Leela absorbed that, pushed her glasses up with one ink-stained knuckle, and went to the bathroom.

Nita set the grocery bag on the counter without unpacking it. "After dinner."

Ben nodded. "Yeah."
